▸ 21.5.7 3

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Classify hypoglycaemia (Level 1/2/3 per ADA/IHSG)
  • [ ] Manage hypoglycaemia in conscious and unconscious patients
  • [ ] Dose glucagon (IM, IN, auto-injector)
  • [ ] Recognise hypoglycaemia unawareness and manage
  • [ ] Prevent recurrent hypoglycaemia

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
DefinitionPlasma glucose <3.9 mmol/L (70 mg/dL) — threshold for neuroendocrine response
Classification (ADA/IHSG 2017)Level 1: <3.9 mmol/L (alert value); Level 2: <3.0 mmol/L (clinically significant); Level 3: Severe (requires assistance)
IncidenceT1DM: 2–3 severe episodes/patient-year; T2DM on insulin: 1–2; T2DM on SU: 0.5–1
Mortality~4–10% of T1DM deaths; "dead in bed" syndrome (nocturnal hypo + arrhythmia)
Risk FactorsInsulin/SU use, CKD, elderly, hypoglycaemia unawareness, tight control, alcohol, exercise, erratic meals

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

LevelGlucoseSymptomsManagement
Level 1 (Alert)<3.9 mmol/LAutonomic: sweating, tremor, palpitations, hunger, anxietyOral carbs 15–20g; recheck 15min
Level 2 (Clinical)<3.0 mmol/LNeuroglycopenic: confusion, drowsiness, speech difficulty, ataxia, visual disturbanceOral carbs if able; glucagon if not
Level 3 (Severe)Any (requires help)Unconscious, seizure, coma — needs 3rd party assistanceGlucagon IM/IN + IV dextrose; emergency services

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

SystemCategoriesKey Features
ADA/IHSGLevel 1: <3.9 mmol/LAutonomic symptoms; self-treat
Level 2: <3.0 mmol/LNeuroglycopenic; impaired judgement
Level 3: SevereRequires assistance; unconscious/seizure
Counter-regulationNormalGlucagon ↑, epinephrine ↑, cortisol/GH ↑
Impaired (T1DM)Glucagon response lost first, then epinephrine blunted → unawareness
UnawarenessAbsent autonomic symptomsSudden neuroglycopenia; risk of severe hypo 6x higher

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

InvestigationRoleKey Details
Capillary/venous glucoseConfirm hypo<3.9 = Level 1; <3.0 = Level 2
CGMDetect nocturnal/unrecognisedTIR/TBR metrics; alerts for <3.9
Whipple's triadDiagnosticSymptoms + low glucose + relief with glucose
Insulin/C-peptideFactitious hypoglycaemiaExogenous insulin → high insulin, low C-peptide; insulinoma → high both

6. Differential Diagnosis

ConditionDistinguishing Features
Factitious hypoglycaemiaSurreptitious insulin/SU use; high insulin, low C-peptide; surreptitious access
InsulinomaFasting hypoglycaemia; high insulin + high C-peptide + high proinsulin; CT/MRI pancreas
Adrenal insufficiencyHypoglycaemia + hypotension + hyponatraemia + hyperkalaemia; cortisol low
Post-bariatric hypoglycaemiaLate dumping (1–3h post-meal); nesidioblastosis; GLP-1 mediated
Non-islet cell tumour hypoglycaemia (NICTH)Large tumours; IGF-2 mediated; low insulin, low C-peptide, low IGF-1

7. Management

Acute Management

ScenarioInterventionDose / Details
Conscious, able to swallowOral carbohydrate15–20g fast-acting carbs (glucose tablets, 150ml juice, 5–6 jelly babies); recheck 15min; repeat if <3.9
Conscious, unable/unwilling to swallowGlucagon1mg IM/SC (adult); 0.5mg <25kg child; Nasal 3mg (Baqsimi); Auto-injector 0.5mg/1mg (Gvoke)
Unconscious / seizureIV dextrose + Glucagon75–80ml 20% dextrose (or 150ml 10%) IV bolus; then 10% dextrose infusion; Glucagon 1mg IM if no IV access
Hospital insulin errorSame as above + root cause analysisInsulin passport; double-check protocol; CGM in hospital

Glucagon Details

FormulationDoseRouteOnsetDuration
GlucaGen HypoKit1mg (adult), 0.5mg (<25kg)IM/SC10–15min60–90min
Baqsimi3mgIntranasal10–15min60–90min
Gvoke (auto-injector)0.5mg/1mgSC10–15min60–90min
Zegalogue (dasiglucagon)0.6mgSC/auto-injector10minReady-to-use, stable at room temp

Prevention of Recurrent Hypoglycaemia

StrategyDetails
Hypoglycaemia unawareness reversalAvoid <3.9 for 2–3 weeks → restores autonomic symptoms; higher targets (HbA1c 58–64), CGM alerts, CSII
Medication adjustmentReduce/stop SU; switch to GLP-1 RA/SGLT2i/DPP-4i; insulin dose reduction; basal-bolus → CSII/AID
EducationCarb counting, sick day rules, alcohol + food, exercise adjustments
TechnologyCGM with predictive alerts; AID systems (reduce hypo by 30–50%)
flowchart TD
    A[Hypoglycaemia Suspected] --> B{Glucose <3.9?}
    B -->|No| C[Not hypoglycaemia]
    B -->|Yes| D{Conscious & able to swallow?}
    D -->|Yes| E[15-20g fast carbs → recheck 15min]
    D -->|No| F{IV access?}
    F -->|Yes| G[75-80ml 20% dextrose IV → 10% dextrose infusion]
    F -->|No| H[Glucagon 1mg IM/SC or Nasal 3mg]
    E --> I{Glucose >3.9 at 15min?}
    I -->|No| E
    I -->|Yes| J[Complex carb snack if next meal >1h]
    G --> K[Monitor glucose q15-30min]
    H --> K

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
ClassificationLevel 1: <3.9; Level 2: <3.0; Level 3: severe (needs help)
Conscious treatment15–20g fast carbs → recheck 15min → repeat if needed
Unconscious/no IVGlucagon 1mg IM/SC (adult), 0.5mg <25kg; Nasal 3mg
Unconscious + IV75–80ml 20% dextrose IV bolus → 10% dextrose infusion
Glucagon typesIM/SC (GlucaGen), IN (Baqsimi), Auto-injector (Gvoke), Dasiglucagon (Zegalogue)
Unawareness reversalAvoid <3.9 for 2–3 weeks → restores glucagon/epinephrine response
Hospital preventionInsulin passport, double-check, CGM, reduce sliding scale

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
How do you classify hypoglycaemia?Level 1: <3.9 mmol/L (alert); Level 2: <3.0 mmol/L (clinically significant); Level 3: Severe — requires assistance (unconscious/seizure)
Management of conscious hypoglycaemia?15–20g fast-acting carbohydrate (glucose tablets, 150ml juice, jelly babies) → recheck 15min → repeat if <3.9 → complex carb snack if meal >1h away
Management of unconscious hypoglycaemia?IV access: 75–80ml 20% dextrose (or 150ml 10%) IV bolus → 10% dextrose infusion. No IV: Glucagon 1mg IM/SC (adult), 0.5mg <25kg; or Nasal 3mg (Baqsimi)
Glucagon dose and routes?1mg IM/SC (adult), 0.5mg <25kg; Nasal 3mg (Baqsimi); Auto-injector 0.5/1mg (Gvoke); Dasiglucagon 0.6mg SC (Zegalogue)
What is hypoglycaemia unawareness?Absent autonomic symptoms due to blunted epinephrine response (glucagon already lost in T1DM); sudden neuroglycopenia; 6x risk severe hypo
How do you reverse hypoglycaemia unawareness?Strict avoidance of glucose <3.9 mmol/L for 2–3 weeks → restores autonomic symptoms; use higher targets, CGM alerts, CSII/AID
What is the "dead in bed" syndrome?Nocturnal hypoglycaemia → cardiac arrhythmia (QT prolongation) → sudden death in young T1DM; prevention: CGM nocturnal alerts, avoid tight control at bedtime

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
Glucagon in starvation/alcoholINEFFECTIVE — requires hepatic glycogen stores; use IV dextrose
Glucagon repeat doseNOT recommended — depletes glycogen; if no response in 15min → IV dextrose
Level 3 definition"Requires assistance" — NOT defined by specific glucose value

Mnemonic: HYPO-15

  • Hypoglycaemia: <3.9 mmol/L
  • You check glucose
  • Patient conscious? → 15–20g carbs
  • Oral carbs: glucose tabs, juice, jelly babies
  • 15min recheck
  • 5 → if still low, repeat 15g
  • Unconscious: No IV → Glucagon 1mg IM/IN; IV → 20% dextrose 75ml
  • Unawareness: avoid <3.9 ×2-3 weeks → restores symptoms
  • Glucagon: needs glycogen (fails in starvation/alcohol)

▼  Show infographic image click to expand
▸ 21.5.8 Insulin Error
[!info]
Inpatient hypoglycaemia = 10–20% of diabetes admissions; insulin errors = leading preventable cause (wrong dose, wrong insulin, wrong timing, sliding scale misuse); protocolised regimens + capillary glucose monitoring + staff education reduce events.

1. Learning Objectives

By the end of this note you should be able to:

  • [ ] Define inpatient hypoglycaemia thresholds
  • [ ] Identify common insulin prescribing/administration errors
  • [ ] Apply prevention strategies (protocols, prescriptions, monitoring)
  • [ ] Manage inpatient hypoglycaemia per severity
  • [ ] Report and review incidents (learning culture)

2. Definitions (ADA / NICE / JBDS)

CategoryCapillary / Venous GlucoseClinical Context
Level 1 (Alert)3.9–3.0 mmol/L (70–54 mg/dL)Asymptomatic or mild symptoms; intervention needed
Level 2 (Clinically Significant)<3.0 mmol/L (<54 mg/dL)Neuroglycopenic symptoms; urgent treatment
Level 3 (Severe)<2.0 mmol/L (<40 mg/dL) OR requires assistanceAltered consciousness, seizure, coma; medical emergency

Inpatient target: Avoid <4.0 mmol/L; treat at 3.9 mmol/L.

3. Common Insulin Errors in Hospital

Error TypeExamplesPrevention
Wrong dose10 units instead of 1; 40 units instead of 4; decimal pointUnit-specific syringes; double-check; no "U" abbreviation (write "units")
Wrong insulinRapid given instead of basal; basal instead of rapid; concentrated (U200/U300) ≠ U100Colour-coded labels; tall-man lettering (HumUlin vs HumAlog); separate storage
Wrong timingRapid insulin given without meal; basal given at wrong time; pre-meal given post-mealLink insulin to meals on chart; pre-meal = 15–20 min before; meal tray verification
Sliding scale misuseSliding scale AS ONLY REGIMEN (no basal); excessive correction doses; no meal bolusBasal-bolus mandatory; sliding scale supplemental only; max correction dose cap
Omitted doseBasal insulin held for "low glucose" (e.g., 5.5) without reviewNever omit basal without senior review; reduce by 20–50% instead
Duplicate doseNurse + patient both give; shift handover failureElectronic MAR; double-sign for insulin; patient self-administration policy
Renal adjustment failureFull insulin dose in eGFR <30 → prolonged hypoglycaemiaDose reduce per eGFR; pharmacy review
Concentrated insulin errorU200/U300/U500 given as U100 volumeSeparate prescribing; dedicated syringes; volume check

4. High-Risk Clinical Scenarios

ScenarioSpecific Risks
NPO / Fasting (pre-op, procedures)Baseline insulin still needed; reduce basal 20–50%; dextrose infusion if prolonged
Renal impairment (AKI, CKD G4–5)Insulin clearance ↓ → reduce dose 30–50%; monitor q4–6h
Corticosteroids (dexamethasone)Hyperglycaemia day 1–2 → ↑ insulin; then hypoglycaemia as steroid wears off
Enteral/Parenteral NutritionFeed interruption → hypoglycaemia; insulin matched to feed rate
Elderly / Frail / DementiaErratic intake, polypharmacy, renal impairment, autonomic neuropathy
Post-bariatric surgeryAltered anatomy, rapid gastric emptying, dumping → unpredictable glucose
Critical illness (ICU)Stress hyperglycaemia → insulin infusion; target 6–10 mmol/L (NICE-SUGAR)

5. Prevention Strategies (JBDS / NICE)

Prescribing

  • Basal-bolus regimen for all insulin-requiring inpatients (sliding scale supplemental only)
  • Pre-printed / electronic order sets with dose calculators
  • No "U" abbreviation — write "units"
  • Concentrated insulins (U200, U300, U500) — separate prescribing pathway
  • Renal dosing guidelines on chart (eGFR-based)

Administration

  • Double-check by two nurses for insulin (or barcode scanning)
  • Meal-coordinated rapid insulin (pre-meal 15–20 min)
  • Capillary glucose before every insulin dose (unless continuous infusion)
  • Patient self-administration if competent (assessed, documented)

Monitoring

  • Capillary glucose pre-meals, bedtime, 3am (if on insulin)
  • Target 5–10 mmol/L (individualised; tighter in critical care)
  • Hypo alert at 3.9 — inform doctor, review insulin
  • Continuous glucose monitoring (CGM) emerging for inpatients

Systems

  • Hypoglycaemia protocol on every ward (IV dextrose, glucagon, escalation)
  • Mandatory reporting of Level 2/3 events (Datix/Safety system)
  • Monthly audit of hypoglycaemia rates, insulin errors, harm
  • Staff education (induction + annual) — insulin safety, device use

6. Inpatient Hypoglycaemia Treatment

SeverityConscious?Treatment
Level 1 (3.0–3.9)Yes15–20g fast-acting carb (glucose gel, juice, 4–5 dextrose tabs); recheck 15 min
Level 2 (<3.0)Yes20g fast-acting carb + complex carb; recheck 15 min; review insulin
Level 3 (<2.0 or needs help)No / ConfusedIV 20% dextrose 100mL (or 10% 200mL); IM glucagon 1mg if no IV access; recheck 10 min
Seizure/ComaNoIV 20% dextrose 100mL + glucagon 1mg IM; protect airway; senior review; stop insulin infusion

Post-treatment: Observe 30–60 min; recheck glucose; identify cause; adjust regimen.

7. Reporting & Learning

StepAction
ImmediateTreat patient; ensure safety; document on chart
Within 24hReport via incident system (Datix); classify harm (none/low/moderate/severe/death)
Within 72hRoot cause analysis for Level 3 or recurrent Level 2
MonthlyWard/unit report: rates, themes, actions
QuarterlyHospital-wide review; share learning; update protocols

Just culture — focus on system failures, not individual blame.

8. Exam Pearls (FCPS/MRCP)

TopicKey Point
Inpatient hypo definitionLevel 1: 3.0–3.9; Level 2: <3.0; Level 3: <2.0 or needs help
Most common insulin errorWrong dose (especially 10x), wrong insulin, wrong timing
Sliding scaleSupplemental only — never sole regimen
Never omit basalReduce 20–50% if concerned; never hold without review
NPO patientBasal insulin still needed (reduce 20–50%); dextrose if prolonged
Renal impairmentReduce insulin 30–50% (eGFR <30); monitor q4–6h
Concentrated insulinU200/U300/U500 — separate prescribing, dedicated syringes
Level 3 treatmentIV 20% dextrose 100mL + glucagon 1mg IM
ReportingAll Level 2/3 mandatory; root cause for Level 3
Target inpatient glucose5–10 mmol/L (avoid <4.0)

▸ 21.6 Microvascular Complications 14
▸ 21.6.10 Diabetic Nephropathy Vs Other Proteinuric Diseases

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Differentiate DKD from other causes of proteinuria in diabetes
  • [ ] Recognise red flags for non-diabetic kidney disease
  • [ ] Select appropriate investigations (immunology, complement, biopsy)
  • [ ] Apply biopsy indications in diabetes

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
Clinical dilemma~30% of proteinuric diabetes have non-DKD component
Biopsy yieldNon-diabetic pathology in 20–30% of biopsied diabetics with proteinuria
Key principleNot all proteinuria in diabetes = DKD

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

Red Flag for Non-DKDSignificance
Rapid eGFR decline>5 mL/min/1.73m²/year
Active urinary sedimentRBC >10/µL, RBC casts, WBC casts
No diabetic retinopathyEspecially if DM >10y or A3 proteinuria
Systemic symptomsFever, rash, arthralgia, haemoptysis, neuropathy
Low BMI at diagnosisType 2 DM usually overweight
Family historyHereditary kidney disease (Alport, ADPKD, Fabry)
Short DM duration<5y with heavy proteinuria

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

Common Non-DKD Proteinuric Diseases in Diabetes

DiseaseTypical ACRKey FeaturesInvestigations
Hypertensive nephrosclerosisA1–A2Long-standing HTN; no retinopathy; bland sedimentClinical; exclude DKD
Chronic GN (IgA, membranous, FSGS)A2–A3Haematuria, RBC casts; may have low complementAutoantibodies, complement, biopsy
AmyloidosisA3 (nephrotic)Nephrotic syndrome; systemic (cardiac, neuropathy, macroglossia)Congo red +ve; FLC, SAP scan
Multiple myelomaA3 (Bence Jones)CRAB features; Bence Jones proteinuriaUrine protein electrophoresis; FLC, bone marrow
Diabetic AINVariableDrug-induced (NSAIDs, PPI, antibiotics); eosinophiliaDrug history; biopsy
Obstructive uropathyVariableHydronephrosis on US; post-void residualUS bladder/kidneys
Alport syndromeA2→A3FH haematuria/renal failure; hearing loss, ocularGenetic (COL4A5); biopsy
Fabry diseaseA2→A3Angiokeratomas, acroparaesthesia, cornea verticillataα-galactosidase A; genetic (GLA)

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

Stepwise Workup for Atypical Proteinuria

StepInvestigationPurpose
1Urine microscopyRBC casts = glomerulonephritis; WBC casts = AIN/pyelonephritis
2AutoantibodiesANA, dsDNA, ANCA, anti-GBM, C3, C4
3Serum FLC + electrophoresisMyeloma, amyloidosis (AL)
4Complement C3/C4Low C3 = post-strep, MPGN, lupus; Low C4 = cryoglobulinaemia
5Viral serologyHBV, HCV, HIV (membranous, MPGN)
6Renal ultrasoundSize, obstruction, cysts (ADPKD)
7Renal biopsyIf diagnosis unclear or immunosuppression considered

Biopsy Indications in Diabetes

IndicationCriteria
Rapid declineeGFR drop >5 mL/min/yr
Active sedimentRBC >10/µL, RBC/WBC casts
No retinopathyDM >10y with A3 proteinuria
Systemic featuresFever, rash, arthralgia, haemoptysis
Short durationDM <5y with heavy proteinuria
Immunosuppression consideredBefore starting steroids/rituximab/etc.

6. Differential Diagnosis Table

FeatureDKDNon-DKD Proteinuria
RetinopathyUsually present (if DM>10y)Often absent
Proteinuria onsetGradual (years)May be acute/subacute
HaematuriaRareCommon (RBC casts = GN)
eGFR declineSlow (2–4 mL/min/yr)Rapid (>5 mL/min/yr)
Systemic symptomsAbsentMay be present
Biopsy (if done)Kimmelstiel-Wilson, GBM thickeningImmune deposits, amyloid, myeloma casts

7. Management

ScenarioAction
Strong DKD featuresContinue DKD management (ACEi/ARB, SGLT2i, etc.)
Red flags presentComplete workup (above); consider biopsy
Biopsy-proven non-DKDTreat specific disease (immunosuppression for GN, chemo for myeloma, etc.)
Mixed DKD + non-DKDTreat both; DKD management + disease-specific

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
Red flagsRapid eGFR decline, active sediment, no retinopathy, systemic symptoms, FH
Urine microscopyRBC casts = GN; WBC casts = AIN/pyelonephritis
Biopsy indicationsRapid decline, active sediment, no retinopathy, systemic features, short DM duration
Common non-DKDHypertensive nephrosclerosis, GN (IgA, membranous), amyloidosis, myeloma
Not all proteinuria in DM = DKD~20–30% have non-diabetic component

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
What are the red flags suggesting non-diabetic kidney disease in a diabetic with proteinuria?Rapid eGFR decline (>5 mL/min/yr), active urinary sediment (RBC casts), absent retinopathy (esp if DM>10y), systemic symptoms, family history, short DM duration with heavy proteinuria
When do you biopsy a diabetic with proteinuria?Rapid decline >5mL/min/yr, active sediment, absent retinopathy with heavy proteinuria, systemic features suggesting systemic disease, DM duration <5y with A3 proteinuria
What does RBC casts in urine indicate?Glomerulonephritis — not DKD (DKD typically has bland sediment)
How does hypertensive nephrosclerosis differ from DKD?Long-standing HTN, bland sediment, no retinopathy, usually A1–A2 proteinuria
What investigation confirms amyloidosis?Congo red staining (apple-green birefringence) on biopsy; serum free light chains, SAP scan

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
All proteinuria in DM = DKD?NO — 20–30% have non-diabetic component; always check red flags
No retinopathy = no DKD?If DM >10y and no retinopathy with heavy proteinuria → think non-DKD
Biopsy always needed?NO — only if red flags or immunosuppression considered

Mnemonic: DDx-PROTEINURIA-DM

  • DKD red flags: rapid eGFR decline, active sediment, no retinopathy
  • DM duration: <5y + heavy proteinuria = biopsy
  • x (exclude): non-DKD causes
  • Proteinuria: not all in DM = DKD
  • RBC casts = GN (not DKD)
  • Ophthalmoscopy: no retinopathy + DM>10y = red flag
  • Tubular: WBC casts = AIN/pyelonephritis
  • Electrophoresis: myeloma, amyloidosis
  • Immunology: ANA, ANCA, anti-GBM, C3, C4
  • Nephrotic syndrome: consider amyloid, myeloma, membranous
  • Ultrasound: size, cysts, obstruction
  • Renal biopsy: if red flags or immunosuppression considered
  • IgA nephropathy: common GN in DM
  • Amyloidosis: Congo red, FLC
  • Myeloma: Bence Jones, CRAB
  • Diabetic AIN: drugs (NSAIDs, PPI, abx)
  • No retinopathy + proteinuria = think non-DKD

▸ 21.6.11 Diabetic Nephropathy
[!info]
DKD = leading cause of ESRD (40–50% of incident dialysis); KDIGO G+A staging; natural history: hyperfiltration → normoalbuminuria → microalbuminuria (A2) → macroalbuminuria (A3) → declining GFR → ESRD; SGLT2i, GLP-1 RA, finerenone = disease-modifying beyond ACEi/ARB.

1. Learning Objectives

By the end of this note you should be able to:

  • [ ] Describe natural history and pathophysiology of DKD
  • [ ] Stage using KDIGO G (GFR) + A (Albuminuria) categories
  • [ ] Screen and monitor (UACR, eGFR)
  • [ ] Apply disease-modifying therapy (ACEi/ARB, SGLT2i, GLP-1 RA, finerenone)
  • [ ] Differentiate DKD from non-diabetic kidney disease

2. Natural History (Mogensen Model - T1DM; Similar T2DM)

StageGFRAlbuminuriaClinicalDuration
1. Hyperfiltration↑↑ (>120–150)Normal (A1)AsymptomaticDiagnosis – 5y
2. Silent / NormoalbuminuricNormal/HighA1 (<30 mg/g)Asymptomatic5–15y
3. Incipient / MicroalbuminuriaNormalA2 (30–300 mg/g)Earliest detectable10–20y
4. Overt / Macroalbuminuria↓ DecliningA3 (>300 mg/g)Hypertension, oedema15–25y
5. ESRD<15 (G5)VariableUraemia, need RRT20–30y

T2DM: More heterogeneous; normoalbuminuric DKD common (30–50% of DKD); may have retinopathy absent.

3. Pathophysiology

MechanismKey Mediators
HaemodynamicHyperfiltration → glomerular hypertension → afferent/efferent arteriolar dysregulation
MetabolicAGE formation, PKC activation, polyol pathway, hexosamine pathway → oxidative stress
InflammatoryNF-κB, TGF-β, MCP-1, TNF-α → macrophage infiltration, fibrosis
RAAS↑ Ang II → efferent constriction → glomerular hypertension; direct profibrotic
SGLT2-mediatedProximal tubular glucose reabsorption → ↑ distal NaCl delivery → macula densa → afferent dilatation

4. KDIGO Staging (G + A) — Risk Matrix

GFR Categories (G)

GeGFR (mL/min/1.73m²)
G1≥90
G260–89
G3a45–59
G3b30–44
G415–29
G5<15

Albuminuria Categories (A)

AACR (mg/mmol)ACR (mg/g)Term
A1<3<30Normal/mildly increased
A23–3030–300Moderately increased (micro)
A3>30>300Severely increased (macro)

Risk Matrix (Heat Map)

A1 (Green)A2 (Yellow)A3 (Orange/Red)
G1, G2LowModerateHigh
G3aModerateHighVery High
G3bHighVery HighVery High
G4, G5Very HighVery HighVery High

5. Screening & Monitoring

TestFrequencyThreshold for Action
UACR (first morning urine)Annual (T1DM from 5y post-dx; T2DM from dx)A2: ACEi/ARB max, SGLT2i; A3: + finerenone, nephrology
eGFR (CKD-EPI)Annual (6m if <60)G3b: adjust meds; G4: nephrology; G5: RRT planning
BPEvery visit<130/80 (KDIGO <120/80)
HbA1c3–6 monthlyIndividualised target

UACR > eGFR for early detection — A2 precedes GFR decline.

6. Disease-Modifying Therapy — The "Big Four"

Drug ClassAgentsKey TrialsIndication in DKD
ACEi / ARBRamipril, losartan, etc.RENAAL, IDNT, MICRO-HOPEFirst-line; max tolerated dose; ↓ intraglomerular pressure
SGLT2iDapagliflozin, empagliflozin, canagliflozinCREDENCE, DAPA-CKD, EMPA-KIDNEYeGFR ≥20 (dapa/empa); ↓ progression 30–40%; non-diabetic CKD too
GLP-1 RASemaglutide, dulaglutide, liraglutideFLOW, AWARD-7, LEADEReGFR ≥15; ↓ albuminuria, ↓ eGFR decline, CV benefit
FinerenoneFinerenone 10–20mgFIDELIO-DKD, FIGARO-DKDeGFR ≥25, A2/A3; non-steroidal MRA; monitor K+

Additive benefit — each class works via distinct pathways; combination is standard.

7. Management by KDIGO Stage

StageManagement
G1–2, A1Optimise glucose/BP/lipids; annual screen; lifestyle
G1–2, A2ACEi/ARB max dose + SGLT2i (if eGFR ≥20); BP <130/80; GLP-1 RA if T2DM
G1–2, A3Above + finerenone (if eGFR ≥25); nephrology referral
G3a, A2/A3Above + adjust metformin (max 1g BD); avoid NSAIDs; monitor K+/eGFR 3–6m
G3b, A2/A3Above + reduce/stop metformin if <45; SGLT2i still OK (dapa/empa ≥20); nephrology co-care
G4, any ANephrology lead; stop metformin; adjust all renally-cleared drugs; RRT planning; avoid contrast
G5RRT (HD/PD/transplant); palliative option

8. Non-Diabetic Kidney Disease in Diabetes — When to Suspect

ClueSuggests
No retinopathy (especially T1DM)Non-diabetic (e.g., hypertensive nephrosclerosis, GN)
Rapid GFR decline (>5 mL/min/yr)Non-diabetic / superimposed
Active urine sediment (RBC casts, dysmorphic RBC)Glomerulonephritis
Heavy proteinuria (>3g/day) early in courseNephrotic syndrome (MN, FSGS)
Short diabetes duration (<5y T1DM, <10y T2DM) with A3Unlikely DKD
Systemic symptoms (rash, arthralgia, haemoptysis)Vasculitis, SLE, amyloidosis

Renal biopsy if diagnosis uncertain — especially if immunosuppression considered.

9. Exam Pearls (FCPS/MRCP)

TopicKey Point
DKD leading cause ESRD40–50% incident dialysis
Natural history stagesHyperfiltration → microalbuminuria (A2) → macroalbuminuria (A3) → ↓GFR → ESRD
Normoalbuminuric DKD30–50% T2DM; screen with eGFR too
ScreeningUACR + eGFR annual (T1DM from 5y; T2DM from dx)
KDIGO G+A6 GFR categories (G1–5) + 3 Albuminuria (A1–3) = risk matrix
First-lineACEi/ARB max dose (unless K+ >5.5, bilateral RAS)
SGLT2i in DKDeGFR ≥20 (dapagliflozin/empagliflozin); CREDENCE, DAPA-CKD, EMPA-KIDNEY
FinerenoneeGFR ≥25, A2/A3; monitor K+ at 4w
GLP-1 RAeGFR ≥15; FLOW trial renal benefit
No retinopathy + DKDThink non-diabetic kidney disease → biopsy

▸ 21.6.13 Autonomic Neuropathy Cardiovascular Gi Genitourina

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Classify autonomic neuropathy: cardiovascular, gastrointestinal, genitourinary, sudomotor
  • [ ] Diagnose cardiovascular autonomic neuropathy (CAN): orthostatic hypotension, HRV, silent MI
  • [ ] Manage gastroparesis: prokinetics, dietary, gastric emptying study
  • [ ] Manage erectile dysfunction / neurogenic bladder
  • [ ] Apply screening tests: Valsalva, tilt table, HRV, gastric emptying

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
DefinitionAutonomic nerve fibre damage from chronic hyperglycaemia
Prevalence~20–40% diabetes; CAN 20%, gastroparesis 5–12%, ED 30–50% men
Risk FactorsHbA1c, duration, age, DSPN, microvascular complications

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

Cardiovascular (CAN)

FeaturePresentation
Orthostatic hypotension↓SBP ≥20 mmHg or ↓DBP ≥10 mmHg within 3min standing; syncope, falls
Resting tachycardia>100 bpm (early); fixed heart rate (late)
Silent myocardial ischaemiaNo angina despite ischaemia (↓afferent cardiac nerves); ↑sudden cardiac death
Exercise intoleranceBlunted HR response; ↓exercise capacity
HRV↓Time/frequency domain measures

Gastrointestinal

FeaturePresentation
GastroparesisNausea, vomiting (undigested food), early satiety, bloating, erratic glucose, weight loss
Diarrhoea/ConstipationAutonomic dysregulation; nocturnal diarrhoea common
Oesophageal dysmotilityDysphagia, reflux

Genitourinary

FeaturePresentation
Erectile dysfunction30–50% men; autonomic + vascular + psychological
Retrograde ejaculationBladder neck incompetence; infertility
Neurogenic bladderAtonic (retention, overflow) or spastic (frequency, urgency, incontinence); recurrent UTIs

Sudomotor

FeaturePresentation
AnhidrosisDistal (feet/legs); ↓thermoregulation
Gustatory sweatingFacial/neck sweating with eating (Frey's syndrome variant)

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

CAN Staging (Ewing / CAN Subscore)

StageCriteria
EarlyAbnormal HRV (1+ test)
DefiniteOrthostatic hypotension + abnormal HRV
SevereOrthostatic hypotension + resting tachycardia + silent MI + HRV severely reduced

Gastroparesis Grading (GCSI)

GradeSymptoms
MildNausea, early satiety
ModerateVomiting 1–3x/week, weight loss <5%
SevereDaily vomiting, weight loss >5%, hospitalisation

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

Cardiovascular Autonomic Tests (Ewing Battery)

TestNormalAbnormal
HRV (deep breathing)E:I ratio >1.5≤1.5
Valsalva ratio>1.5≤1.2
30:15 ratio>1.04≤1.0
Orthostatic BP↓SBP <10↓SBP ≥20
Sustained handgripDBP rise >16≤10

Gastroparesis

InvestigationDetails
Gastric emptying scintigraphyGold standard: T½ solids >120min (or >40min liquids)
13C-octanoate breath testAlternative; non-radioactive
Wireless motility capsulepH, pressure, transit time

Genitourinary

InvestigationDetails
IIEF-5ED severity score
UrodynamicsNeurogenic bladder type
Post-void residual>150ml = retention

6. Differential Diagnosis

ConditionDistinguishing Features
Autonomic neuropathy (non-diabetic)Amyloid, autoimmune (paraneoplastic), HIV, alcohol, drugs
Medication effectsβ-blockers (mask tachycardia), diuretics (orthostasis), anticholinergics
Cardiac diseaseHeart failure, arrhythmias (independent of CAN)
GI obstructionMechanical vs gastroparesis (imaging)
Psychogenic EDMorning erections preserved; situational

7. Management

Cardiovascular Autonomic Neuropathy

InterventionDetails
Orthostatic hypotension↑Salt/fluid (2–3L/day), compression stockings, head-up tilt sleep, fludrocortisone 0.1–0.3mg OD, midodrine 2.5–10mg TDS
Silent MI preventionIntensive risk factor control; consider stress testing if high risk
Resting tachycardiaIvabradine if HR >70 on max β-blocker; avoid non-selective β-blockers

Gastroparesis

LineAgentDoseNotes
DietarySmall frequent meals (6–8/day), low fat/fibre, liquid calories—Foundation
1st line prokineticMetoclopramide10mg TDS (max 5 days continuous)D2 antagonist; EPS risk (limit 5d); avoid parkinsonism
Domperidone10mg TDSPeripheral D2; less CNS EPS; cardiac QT risk
Erythromycin250mg TDS (short-term 2–4wk)Motilin agonist; tachyphylaxis; QT
2nd linePrucalopride / TegaserodSpecialist5-HT4 agonists
RefractoryGES (gastric electrical stimulation), pyloroplasty, jejunostomySurgicalSpecialist centres

Erectile Dysfunction

LineIntervention
1stPDE5 inhibitors (sildenafil 50mg, tadalafil 10mg, vardenafil 10mg) — take 30–60min pre-activity
2ndVacuum erection device
3rdIntraurethral alprostadil (MUSE)
4thIntracavernosal alprostadil injection
5thPenile prosthesis

Neurogenic Bladder

TypeManagement
Atonic (retention)Clean intermittent catheterisation (CIC); bethanechol 10–25mg TDS
Spastic (urgency)Anticholinergics (oxybutynin, tolterodine); botulinum toxin A; sacral neuromodulation
flowchart TD
    A[Autonomic Neuropathy] --> B{System}
    B -->|CV| C[Orthostatic HTN: fludrocortisone/midodrine]
    C --> D[Silent MI: risk factor control]
    B -->|GI| E[Gastroparesis: diet → metoclopramide/domperidone/erythromycin]
    B -->|GU| F[ED: PDE5i → vacuum → alprostadil → prosthesis]
    F --> G[Bladder: CIC (atonic) / anticholinergics (spastic)]
    B -->|Sudomotor| H[Anhidrosis: avoid heat; gustatory sweating: reassure]

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
CANOrthostatic hypotension (↓SBP≥20), resting tachycardia, silent MI, HRV ↓
Orthostatic hypotension RxFludrocortisone 0.1–0.3mg OD, midodrine 2.5–10mg TDS, compression stockings
GastroparesisNausea, vomiting, erratic glucose; gastric emptying T½ >120min; metoclopramide/domperidone/erythromycin
EDPDE5i 1st line; vacuum → alprostadil → prosthesis
Neurogenic bladderCIC for retention; anticholinergics/botox for urgency
ScreeningAnnual: orthostatic BP, HRV (if available), symptom questionnaire

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
What is cardiovascular autonomic neuropathy (CAN)?Orthostatic hypotension (↓SBP≥20), resting tachycardia >100bpm, silent MI, reduced HRV, exercise intolerance
How do you diagnose orthostatic hypotension?↓SBP ≥20 mmHg or ↓DBP ≥10 mmHg within 3 minutes of standing
How do you manage orthostatic hypotension?Non-drug: ↑salt/fluid, compression stockings, head-up tilt; Drug: fludrocortisone 0.1–0.3mg OD, midodrine 2.5–10mg TDS
What is gastroparesis and how do you diagnose it?Delayed gastric emptying without obstruction; symptoms: nausea, vomiting, early satiety, erratic glucose; Gastric emptying scintigraphy: T½ solids >120min
What are the prokinetics for gastroparesis?Metoclopramide 10mg TDS (max 5d, EPS risk), Domperidone 10mg TDS (less EPS, QT risk), Erythromycin 250mg TDS (short-term, tachyphylaxis)
How do you manage erectile dysfunction in diabetes?1st: PDE5 inhibitors (sildenafil 50mg, tadalafil 10mg); 2nd: vacuum device; 3rd: intraurethral/intracavernosal alprostadil; 4th: penile prosthesis
What is a silent MI in diabetes?Myocardial ischaemia/infarction without typical angina due to cardiac autonomic denervation; ↑sudden cardiac death risk

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
CAN = all autonomic?NO — CAN = cardiovascular only; autonomic includes GI, GU, sudomotor
Metoclopramide long-term?NO — max 5 days continuous (EPS risk); use domperidone for chronic
PDE5i with nitrates?CONTRAINDICATED — severe hypotension

Mnemonic: AUTO-NOMIC

  • Autonomic: CV, GI, GU, Sudomotor
  • Upright: orthostatic hypotension (↓SBP≥20) → fludrocortisone/midodrine
  • Tachycardia: resting >100 (early CAN)
  • Orthostatic: ↓SBP≥20 → midodrine/fludrocortisone
  • Nausea/vomiting: gastroparesis (T½>120min)
  • Oesophageal: dysmotility
  • Medication: metoclopramide (5d max), domperidone, erythromycin
  • Impotence: PDE5i → vacuum → alprostadil → prosthesis
  • Catheterisation: CIC for atonic bladder
  • Neurogenic bladder: anticholinergics for urgency, CIC for retention

▸ 21.6.14 Distal Symmetric Polyneuropathy Dspn

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Diagnose DSPN: stocking-glove sensory loss, absent ankle reflexes
  • [ ] Differentiate painful vs painless DSPN
  • [ ] Apply Toronto Clinical Scoring System for severity
  • [ ] Manage neuropathic pain (duloxetine/pregabalin/gabapentin/amitriptyline)
  • [ ] Exclude non-diabetic mimics (B12, alcohol, CIDP)

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
DefinitionSymmetric, length-dependent sensorimotor polyneuropathy from chronic hyperglycaemia
Prevalence~50% T2DM at 20y; ~20% T1DM at 20y
Risk FactorsHbA1c, duration, age, HTN, dyslipidaemia, smoking, height
PathophysiologyPolyol pathway, AGEs, PKC-β, oxidative stress → axonal degeneration (dying-back)

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

FeaturePainful DSPN (10–20%)Painless DSPN
SensoryBurning, shooting, lancinating, allodynia, hyperalgesiaNumbness, "cotton wool" sensation, insensate foot
Nocturnal worseningYes (often wakes patient)N/A
ReflexesAnkle reflexes absent; knee preservedAnkle reflexes absent
MotorLate: mild distal weakness, wastingLate: mild weakness
AutonomicMay coexistMay coexist

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

Toronto Clinical Scoring System (TCSS)

ComponentPoints
SymptomsNumbness 1, Tinling 1, Pain 1, Weakness 1, Ataxia 1
ReflexesAnkle absent 1, Knee absent 1
SensationPinprick ↓ 1, Vibration ↓ 1, Temperature ↓ 1
ScoreInterpretation
0–5No / mild DSPN
6–8Moderate DSPN
≥9Severe DSPN

NCS Findings (if performed)

ParameterDSPN Pattern
Sensory nerve action potential (SNAP)↓Amplitude (sural, radial)
Motor NCVNormal or mildly ↓
CMAP amplitudeNormal or ↓ (severe)
F-wavesProlonged/absent
PatternAxonal > demyelinating; length-dependent

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

InvestigationRole
10g monofilamentScreening (LOPS); 6 sites/foot
128Hz tuning forkVibration perception; <10s at hallux = abnormal
ANNSSymptom questionnaire (score ≥3 = likely DSPN)
NCSConfirm atypical/mononeuropathy/radiculoplexus; not routine for typical DSPN
Bloods (exclude mimics)B12, folate, TSH, ESR, SPEP, HIV, ANA, ANCA, alcohol history

6. Differential Diagnosis

ConditionDistinguishing Features
B12 deficiencyMacrocytic anaemia, subacute combined degeneration (posterior column), +ve IF Ab
Alcoholic neuropathyPainful, sensory > motor; improves with abstinence + thiamine
CIDPProgressive >8 weeks, proximal + distal weakness, areflexia, ↑CSF protein, demyelinating NCS
Vasculitic neuropathyMononeuritis multiplex, systemic features, ↑ESR/CRP, ANCA+
Amyloid neuropathyAutonomic dominant, carpal tunnel, family history, Congo red +ve
HypothyroidCarpal tunnel, myxoedema, delayed reflex relaxation

7. Management

Neuropathic Pain (NICE / ADA / NeuPSIG Aligned)

LineAgentDoseNotes
1stDuloxetine30mg OD → 60mg OD (max 120mg)SNRI; also helps depression; avoid hepatic impairment
Pregabalin75mg BD → 150–300mg BD (max 600mg)Renal adjust; weight gain, dizziness, euphoria risk
Gabapentin300mg TDS → 600–1200mg TDS (max 3600mg)Renal adjust; slower titration
Amitriptyline10mg ON → 25–75mg ONTCA; anticholinergic SE; avoid elderly/CVD
2ndTramadol / TapentadolPRN breakthroughOpioid risk; short-term only
3rdCapsaicin 8% patchQ3moTopical; application pain
Lidocaine 5% plasterDaily 12h on/offLocalised pain

Non-Pharmacological

InterventionEvidence
Glycaemic controlDCCT: ↓DSPN 60% (T1DM); UKPDS: ↓microvascular (T2DM)
ExerciseImproves nerve conduction, pain, QoL
Foot careDaily inspection; offloading if LOPS; podiatry referral
TENS / AcupunctureLimited evidence; adjunctive

Renal Dosing for Pregabalin/Gabapentin

eGFR (mL/min)PregabalinGabapentin
≥60150–600mg/day900–3600mg/day
30–5975–300mg/day400–1400mg/day
15–2925–150mg/day200–700mg/day
<1525–75mg/day100–350mg/day
flowchart TD
    A[DSPN Diagnosed] --> B{Painful?}
    B -->|No| C[Reassure + foot care + glycaemic control]
    B -->|Yes| D[1st line: Duloxetine/Pregabalin/Gabapentin/Amitriptyline]
    D --> E{Renal impairment?}
    E -->|Yes| F[Renal dose adjust pregabalin/gabapentin]
    E -->|No| G[Standard titration]
    F --> H[Review 4-6 weeks]
    G --> H
    H --> I{Adequate relief?}
    I -->|No| J[Switch 1st line agent OR add 2nd line]
    I -->|Yes| K[Continue + monitor 3-monthly]

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
DSPNStocking-glove sensory loss, absent ankle reflexes; painful 10–20%
ScreeningAnnual: 10g monofilament + vibration + ANNS
Toronto Score0–5 mild, 6–8 mod, ≥9 severe
Pain Rx 1st lineDuloxetine, Pregabalin, Gabapentin, Amitriptyline
Renal adjustPregabalin/Gabapentin — MUST adjust for eGFR
Glycaemic controlDCCT/UKPDS: intensive ↓DSPN
MimicsB12, alcohol, CIDP, vasculitis — exclude if atypical

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
What is the typical presentation of DSPN?Stocking-glove sensory loss (pinprick, temperature, vibration, proprioception), absent ankle reflexes; painful 10–20% (burning, nocturnal)
How do you screen for DSPN?Annual: 10g monofilament (6 sites/foot — cannot feel = LOPS), 128Hz tuning fork on hallux IP (<10s abnormal), ANNS questionnaire
What are the 1st line agents for painful DSPN?Duloxetine (30→60mg), Pregabalin (75→150–300mg BD), Gabapentin (300→600–1200mg TDS), Amitriptyline (10→25–75mg ON)
How do you adjust pregabalin/gabapentin in CKD?MUST renal adjust: Pregabalin eGFR 30–60: 75–150mg/day; 15–30: 25–75mg/day; <15: 25mg/day. Gabapentin similar.
What is the Toronto Clinical Scoring System?Symptoms (numbness, tingling, pain, weakness, ataxia) + reflexes (ankle, knee) + sensation (pinprick, vibration, temp); 0–5 mild, 6–8 mod, ≥9 severe
How does intensive glycaemic control affect DSPN?DCCT: ↓DSPN 60% in T1DM; UKPDS: ↓microvascular in T2DM
What non-diabetic conditions mimic DSPN?B12 deficiency, alcoholic neuropathy, CIDP, vasculitis, amyloid, hypothyroidism — exclude if atypical (asymmetric, rapid, proximal, systemic)

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
All painful feet = DSPN?NO — consider plantar fasciitis, Morton's neuroma, PAD, Charcot
DSPN = autonomic neuropathy?NO — can coexist but distinct; DSPN = sensory/motor; Autonomic = CV/GI/GU
Pregabalin = gabapentin?Similar but pregabalin: linear PK, faster onset, less dose variability; both need renal adjust

Mnemonic: DSPN-STOCKING

  • DSPN: length-dependent, dying-back axonal
  • Stocking-glove: toes → up, fingers → up
  • Painful: 10-20% (burning, nocturnal)
  • Numb: painless = insensate foot → ulcer risk
  • Screening: 10g monofilament + 128Hz tuning fork + ANNS
  • Toronto Score: 0-5 mild, 6-8 mod, ≥9 severe
  • Oral meds 1st line: Duloxetine, Pregabalin, Gabapentin, Amitriptyline
  • CKD: renal adjust pregabalin/gabapentin
  • Ketogenic: not relevant
  • Intensive glycaemic control: DCCT 60%, UKPDS ↓DSPN
  • Neuritis mimics: B12, alcohol, CIDP, vasculitis
  • Gait: ataxia from proprioception loss

▸ 21.6.15 Neuropathic Pain Management

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Apply stepwise pharmacological management of painful DSPN
  • [ ] Select and titrate 1st line agents (duloxetine, pregabalin, gabapentin, amitriptyline)
  • [ ] Apply renal dosing for pregabalin/gabapentin
  • [ ] Manage refractory pain (combination, opioids, topical, interventional)
  • [ ] Recognise and manage adverse effects

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
Painful DSPN10–20% of DSPN; burning, shooting, lancinating, allodynia, hyperalgesia
Impact↓QoL, sleep disturbance, depression, anxiety, ↓function
GuidelinesNICE CG173, NeuPSIG, ADA, AAN — broadly aligned on 1st line agents

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

Pain CharacteristicDescription
BurningConstant, distally predominant
Shooting/LancinatingParoxysmal, electric shock-like
AllodyniaPain from non-painful stimulus (light touch, bedsheets)
HyperalgesiaExaggerated pain response
Nocturnal worseningCommon; disrupts sleep

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

Neuropathic Pain Grading (NeuPSIG)

GradeCriteria
PossibleHistory suggestive + neuroanatomically plausible distribution
ProbablePossible + sensory signs in same distribution
DefiniteProbable + confirmatory test (NCS, QST, skin biopsy)

Pain Severity (Numeric Rating Scale)

ScoreSeverity
1–3Mild
4–6Moderate
7–10Severe

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

InvestigationRole
Clinical examSensory signs (stocking-glove), allodynia mapping
NCSConfirm DSPN; exclude mononeuropathy/radiculoplexus
QST (Quantitative Sensory Testing)Thermal/mechanical thresholds; research/specialist
Skin biopsyIntraepidermal nerve fibre density (IENFD); specialist
Pain scalesNRS (0–10), DN4, PainDETECT, MSPQ

6. Differential Diagnosis

ConditionDistinguishing Features
Mechanical foot painPlantar fasciitis (heel, morning), Morton's neuroma (interdigital), PAD (claudication)
CharcotHot swollen foot, deformity, preserved pulses
OsteomyelitisUlcer + probe-to-bone, elevated inflammatory markers
RadiculopathyDermatomal distribution, back pain, MRI

7. Management

Stepwise Pharmacological Algorithm (NICE / NeuPSIG / ADA)

flowchart TD
    A[Painful DSPN] --> B[1st Line: Duloxetine / Pregabalin / Gabapentin / Amitriptyline]
    B --> C{4-6 week trial at max tolerated dose}
    C -->|Effective| D[Continue + review 3-monthly]
    C -->|Ineffective/Intolerant| E[Switch to different 1st line agent]
    E --> F{Effective?}
    F -->|Yes| D
    F -->|No| G[Combination 1st line agents]
    G --> H{Effective?}
    H -->|Yes| D
    H -->|No| I[2nd Line: Tramadol/Tapentadol PRN + specialist referral]
    I --> J{Effective?}
    J -->|No| K[3rd Line: Capsaicin 8% patch / Lidocaine 5% plaster / Spinal cord stimulation]

1st Line Agents (Monotherapy)

AgentStarting DoseTitrationMax DoseKey SE
Duloxetine30mg OD60mg OD at 1–2wk120mg/dayNausea, dry mouth, ↑BP, sexual dysfunction
Pregabalin75mg BD150mg BD at 1wk → 300mg BD600mg/dayDizziness, weight gain, euphoria, renal adjust
Gabapentin300mg TDS600mg TDS at 3d → 1200mg TDS3600mg/dayDizziness, somnolence, renal adjust
Amitriptyline10mg ON25mg ON at 1wk → 50–75mg ON75mg ONAnticholinergic, sedation, orthostatic HTN, avoid elderly/CVD

Renal Dosing (Critical for Exams)

eGFR (mL/min)Pregabalin (mg/day)Gabapentin (mg/day)
≥60150–600900–3600
30–5975–300400–1400
15–2925–150200–700
<1525–75100–350

Combination Therapy

CombinationRationale
Duloxetine + PregabalinDifferent mechanisms (SNRI + α2δ); additive; ↑SE
Amitriptyline + PregabalinDifferent mechanisms; monitor anticholinergic + sedation

2nd Line

AgentIndicationCaution
TramadolPRN breakthroughOpioid; seizure risk; serotonin syndrome with duloxetine
TapentadolPRN breakthroughµ-opioid + NA reuptake; less constipation

3rd Line / Specialist

InterventionDetails
Capsaicin 8% patchQ3 months; application pain; specialist application
Lidocaine 5% plaster12h on/12h off; localised neuropathic pain
Spinal cord stimulationRefractory; trial then implant; NICE TA159
Peripheral nerve field stimulationEmerging

Non-Pharmacological

InterventionEvidence
TENSLow-quality evidence; adjunct
AcupunctureLimited; some QoL benefit
CBT / ACTPain coping, sleep, mood
ExerciseImproves nerve conduction, pain, function

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
1st lineDuloxetine, Pregabalin, Gabapentin, Amitriptyline — 4–6 week trial
Renal dosingPregabalin/Gabapentin MUST adjust by eGFR (table above)
AmitriptylineAvoid elderly, CVD, glaucoma; anticholinergic burden
CombinationDuloxetine + Pregabalin (different mechanisms)
2nd lineTramadol/Tapentadol PRN (opioid risks)
3rd lineCapsaicin 8% patch, Lidocaine 5% plaster, SCS (refractory)
Non-drugCBT, exercise, TENS, acupuncture (adjunct)

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
What are the 1st line agents for painful diabetic neuropathy?Duloxetine, Pregabalin, Gabapentin, Amitriptyline (NICE/NeuPSIG/ADA aligned)
How do you titrate pregabalin?Start 75mg BD → 150mg BD at 1 week → 300mg BD; max 600mg/day; renal adjust essential
How do you adjust gabapentin/pregabalin in renal impairment?MUST adjust: Pregabalin eGFR 30–60: 75–300mg/day; 15–30: 25–150mg/day; <15: 25–75mg/day. Gabapentin similar.
When do you use amitriptyline?1st line option; avoid elderly, CVD, glaucoma, urinary retention; start 10mg ON → 25–75mg ON
What is the role of opioids in neuropathic pain?2nd line only (Tramadol/Tapentadol PRN); short-term; avoid long-term; specialist if considering
When do you refer for spinal cord stimulation?Refractory to 1st/2nd line; specialist assessment; NICE TA159

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
All 1st line equally effective?Similar efficacy (NNT ~4–5); choose by comorbidity/SE profile
Gabapentin = pregabalin?Similar but pregabalin: linear PK, faster onset, less variability; both need renal adjust
Amitriptyline safe in elderly?NO — anticholinergic burden, falls, orthostatic HTN, arrhythmia risk

Mnemonic: PAIN-DSPN-RX

  • Painful DSPN: 10-20% of DSPN
  • Agents 1st line: Duloxetine, Pregabalin, Gabapentin, Amitriptyline
  • Initial trial: 4-6 weeks max tolerated dose
  • Next: switch 1st line if fail → combine → 2nd line
  • Duloxetine: SNRI, 30→60mg, also depression
  • Spinal cord stim: 3rd line refractory
  • Pregabalin: renal adjust (eGFR 30-60: 75-300, 15-30: 25-150, <15: 25-75)
  • Neuropathic: burning, shooting, allodynia
  • Renal dosing: MUST for pregabalin/gabapentin
  • X (no): amitriptyline in elderly/CVD

#medicine #diabetes #davidson #fcps #mrcp #full-fcps-mrcp-note

▼  Show infographic image click to expand
▸ 21.6.16 Diabetic Neuropathy
[!info]
Diabetic neuropathy: most common diabetic complication — DSPN (distal symmetric polyneuropathy, 50% T2DM); Autonomic (CV: orthostatic hypotension, silent MI; GI: gastroparesis; GU: ED, bladder); Mononeuropathies (cranial, median, femoral); Screening (10g monofilament, 128Hz tuning fork, ANNS); Pain (duloxetine/pregabalin/gabapentin/amitriptyline 1st line).

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Classify diabetic neuropathy types (DSPN, autonomic, mononeuropathy, radiculoplexus)
  • [ ] Perform bedside screening (10g monofilament, vibration, ANNS)
  • [ ] Diagnose autonomic neuropathy subtypes (CV, GI, GU)
  • [ ] Manage neuropathic pain (duloxetine/pregabalin/gabapentin/amitriptyline)
  • [ ] Differentiate from non-diabetic causes (B12, alcohol, CIDP, vasculitis)

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
DefinitionNerve damage from chronic hyperglycaemia: metabolic (polyol pathway, AGEs, PKC), vascular (endoneurial ischaemia), oxidative stress
PrevalenceDSPN: ~50% T2DM, ~20% T1DM at 20y; Autonomic: ~20–40%; Painful DSPN: 10–20% of DSPN
Risk FactorsHbA1c, duration, age, hypertension, dyslipidaemia, smoking, alcohol, height, HLA-DR3/4
PathophysiologyPolyol pathway (sorbitol accumulation) → osmotic swelling; AGEs → protein cross-linking; PKC-β → vascular dysfunction; oxidative stress → mitochondrial dysfunction

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

TypeFrequencyKey Features
DSPNMost commonStocking-glove sensory loss (pinprick, temperature, vibration, proprioception); absent ankle reflexes; painful (burning, shooting, lancinating) in 10–20%; nocturnal worsening
Autonomic CV20–40%Resting tachycardia (>100bpm), orthostatic hypotension (↓SBP ≥20/DBP ≥10), exercise intolerance, silent MI, HRV ↓
Autonomic GI20–30%Gastroparesis: nausea, vomiting, early satiety, bloating, erratic glucose; constipation/diarrhoea
Autonomic GU30–50% menErectile dysfunction (autonomic + vascular); retrograde ejaculation; neurogenic bladder (retention, incontinence, UTIs)
Mononeuropathies<5%Cranial (III > VI > IV) — ptosis, diplopia, pupil sparing; Median (CTS); Ulnar; Peroneal (foot drop); Femoral
RadiculoplexusRareSevere pain → weakness/wasting (lumbosacral > cervical); asymmetric; weight loss; self-limiting 6–18mo

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

ClassificationSubtypesKey Features
DSPNPainful vs painlessPainful: burning, allodynia, hyperalgesia; Painless: insensate foot → ulcer risk
AutonomicCardiovascularOrthostatic hypotension, resting tachycardia, silent ischaemia, HRV ↓
GastrointestinalGastroparesis, diarrhoea, constipation
GenitourinaryED, retrograde ejaculation, neurogenic bladder
SudomotorAnhidrosis (distal), gustatory sweating
MononeuropathyCranial III/IV/VI, median, ulnar, peroneal, femoralAcute onset; usually self-limiting 6–12 weeks
Radiculoplexus neuropathyLumbosacral (diabetic amyotrophy), cervicalSevere pain → wasting/weakness; asymmetric; weight loss

DSPN Severity (Toronto Clinical Scoring System)

ScoreComponentsInterpretation
0–5Symptoms, reflexes, sensationNo/mild DSPN
6–8Moderate DSPN
≥9Severe DSPN

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

InvestigationRoleKey Details
10g monofilamentScreening (LOPS)Apply to 6 sites/foot (hallux, 1st/3rd/5th met heads, medial/lateral heel); cannot feel = LOPS; sensitivity 85%, specificity 90%
128Hz tuning forkVibration perceptionOn bony prominence (hallux IP joint, medial malleolus); count seconds; <10s = abnormal
ANNS (Annual Neuropathy Symptom Score)Symptom questionnaire4 questions (pain, numbness, tingling, weakness); score ≥3 = likely DSPN
Nerve conduction studies (NCS)Confirmation / atypical↓Amplitude, ↓conduction velocity; axonal > demyelinating; if atypical/mononeuropathy/radiculoplexus
HRV / Valsalva / tilt tableAutonomic CVHRV ↓, Valsalva ratio <1.2, orthostatic BP drop
Gastric emptying studyGastroparesisT½ >120min (solids) or >40min (liquids); scintigraphy gold standard
Blood testsExclude mimicsB12, folate, TSH, ESR, SPEP, HIV, ANA, ANCA, alcohol history
Screening Algorithm: Annual 10g monofilament + vibration/ANNS in all DM; if abnormal → refer podiatry/neurology

6. Differential Diagnosis

ConditionDistinguishing Features
B12 deficiencyMacrocytic anaemia, subacute combined degeneration (posterior column), +ve intrinsic factor Ab; MMA/homocysteine ↑
Alcoholic neuropathyPainful, predominantly sensory; history; improves with abstinence + thiamine
CIDPProgressive >8 weeks, proximal + distal weakness, areflexia, ↑CSF protein, demyelinating NCS
Vasculitic neuropathyMononeuritis multiplex, systemic features, ↑ESR/CRP, ANCA+, nerve biopsy
Amyloid neuropathyAutonomic dominant, carpal tunnel, family history, Congo red +ve biopsy
Hypothyroid neuropathyCarpal tunnel, myxoedema, delayed reflex relaxation; TSH ↑

7. Management

Neuropathic Pain (1st Line → 2nd Line → 3rd Line)

LineAgentDoseNotes
1stDuloxetine30mg OD → 60mg OD (max 120mg)SNRI; also helps depression; avoid if hepatic impairment
Pregabalin75mg BD → 150–300mg BD (max 600mg)Renal adjust; weight gain, dizziness, euphoria risk
Gabapentin300mg TDS → 600–1200mg TDS (max 3600mg)Renal adjust; slower titration
Amitriptyline10mg ON → 25–75mg ONTCA; anticholinergic SE; avoid elderly/CVD
2ndTramadol / TapentadolPRN breakthroughOpioid risk; short-term only
3rdCapsaicin 8% patchQ3moTopical; application pain
Lidocaine 5% plasterDaily 12h on/offLocalised pain

Autonomic Neuropathy Management

SubtypeManagement
Orthostatic hypotension↑Salt/fluid, compression stockings, head-up tilt sleep, fludrocortisone 0.1–0.3mg OD, midodrine 2.5–10mg TDS
GastroparesisSmall frequent meals, low fat/fibre, metoclopramide 10mg TDS (max 5d), domperidone 10mg TDS, erythromycin 250mg TDS (short-term)
Erectile dysfunctionPDE5 inhibitors (sildenafil 50mg, tadalafil 10mg); vacuum device; intraurethral alprostadil; penile prosthesis
Neurogenic bladderTimed voiding, CIC, anticholinergics (oxybutynin), botulinum toxin, sacral neuromodulation

Glycaemic Control for Prevention

TargetEvidence
HbA1c <53mmol/mol (7.0%)DCCT: ↓DSPN 60%, autonomic 45% in T1DM; UKPDS: ↓microvascular in T2DM
Avoid glucose variabilityCGM: CV <36%; glycaemic variability linked to neuropathy
flowchart TD
    A[Diabetic Neuropathy Suspected] --> B{Type?}
    B -->|DSPN| C[Screen: 10g monofilament + vibration + ANNS]
    B -->|Autonomic CV| D[Orthostatic BP, HRV, resting HR]
    B -->|Autonomic GI| E[Gastric emptying study]
    B -->|Autonomic GU| F[Urodynamics, IIEF]
    B -->|Mononeuropathy| G[NCS - confirm localisation]
    C --> H{LOPS?}
    H -->|Yes| I[Refer podiatry/foot clinic]
    H -->|No| J[Annual rescreen]
    C --> K{Painful?}
    K -->|Yes| L[1st line: Duloxetine/Pregabalin/Gabapentin/Amitriptyline]
    K -->|No| M[Reassure + foot care education]

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
DSPNStocking-glove sensory loss, absent ankle reflexes; painful 10–20% (burning, nocturnal)
ScreeningAnnual: 10g monofilament (6 sites/foot) + 128Hz tuning fork + ANNS; LOPS = cannot feel monofilament
Autonomic CVOrthostatic hypotension (↓SBP≥20), resting tachycardia, silent MI, HRV ↓
Autonomic GIGastroparesis: nausea, vomiting, early satiety, erratic glucose; gastric emptying T½ >120min
Autonomic GUED (50% men), retrograde ejaculation, neurogenic bladder
MononeuropathiesCranial III (ptosis, diplopia, pupil sparing), median (CTS), peroneal (foot drop); self-limiting 6–12wk
Pain management1st line: Duloxetine, Pregabalin, Gabapentin, Amitriptyline; renal adjust pregabalin/gabapentin
Glycaemic controlDCCT/UKPDS: intensive control ↓DSPN 60% (T1DM), ↓microvascular (T2DM)
Non-diabetic mimicsB12 deficiency, alcohol, CIDP, vasculitis, amyloid — exclude if atypical

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
How do you screen for diabetic neuropathy?Annual: 10g monofilament (6 sites: hallux, 1st/3rd/5th met heads, medial/lateral heel — cannot feel = LOPS), 128Hz tuning fork on hallux IP joint/medial malleolus (<10s abnormal), ANNS questionnaire
What is the stocking-glove distribution?Distal symmetric sensory loss starting in toes/feet → progresses proximally; follows longest nerve fibres first
How do you classify autonomic neuropathy?Cardiovascular (orthostatic hypotension, silent MI, HRV), GI (gastroparesis), GU (ED, bladder), sudomotor
What is gastroparesis and how do you diagnose it?Delayed gastric emptying without obstruction; symptoms: nausea, vomiting, early satiety, bloating, erratic glucose; Gastric emptying scintigraphy: T½ solids >120min, liquids >40min
What are the 1st line agents for painful DSPN?Duloxetine (30→60mg), Pregabalin (75→150–300mg BD), Gabapentin (300→600–1200mg TDS), Amitriptyline (10→25–75mg ON); renal adjust pregabalin/gabapentin
How does cranial nerve III palsy present in diabetes?Acute ptosis, ophthalmoplegia (down and out), pupil sparing (parasympathetic fibres peripheral), pain common; self-limiting 6–12wk
What is diabetic amyotrophy?Lumbosacral radiculoplexus neuropathy: severe hip/thigh pain → wasting/weakness, asymmetric, weight loss; self-limiting 6–18mo
How do you manage orthostatic hypotension in autonomic neuropathy?Non-drug: ↑salt/fluid, compression stockings, head-up tilt; Drug: fludrocortisone 0.1–0.3mg OD, midodrine 2.5–10mg TDS

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
DSPN = autonomic neuropathy?NO — DSPN = distal symmetric sensory ± motor; Autonomic = CV/GI/GU/sudomotor; often coexist but distinct
All painful neuropathy = diabetic?NO — exclude B12, alcohol, CIDP, vasculitis if atypical (asymmetric, rapid, proximal, systemic)
Pregabalin/gabapentin dosing in CKD?Must renal adjust: Pregabalin eGFR 30–60: 75–150mg/day; 15–30: 25–75mg/day; <15: 25mg/day. Gabapentin similar.

Mnemonic: NEURO-PATHY

  • Neuropathy types: DSPN, Autonomic, Mono, Radiculoplexus
  • Exam: 10g monofilament (6 sites) + 128Hz tuning fork + ANNS
  • Ulcers from LOPS (loss of protective sensation)
  • Radiculoplexus: amyotrophy (pain → wasting)
  • Orthostatic hypotension: ↓SBP≥20, fludrocortisone/midodrine
  • Painful DSPN: 10-20%; 1st line duloxetine/pregabalin/gabapentin/amitriptyline
  • Autonomic: CV (silent MI), GI (gastroparesis), GU (ED)
  • Tuning fork 128Hz <10s abnormal
  • HbA1c <53 prevents (DCCT 60%, UKPDS)
  • Yield NCS if atypical/mono/radiculoplexus

DefinitionNerve damage from hyperglycaemia: metabolic, vascular, oxidative

▸ 21.6.17 Retinopathy Treatment Laser Anti Vegf Vitrectomy
[!info]
Treatment triad: PRP (PDR gold standard), Anti-VEGF (CI-DMO first-line; PDR alternative), Vitrectomy (non-clearing VH, TRD, NVG); laser types: PRP (pan-retinal) vs focal/grid (macular); systemic optimisation essential adjunct.

1. Learning Objectives

By the end of this note you should be able to:

  • [ ] Differentiate PRP vs focal/grid laser indications & parameters
  • [ ] Apply anti-VEGF protocols for DMO and PDR
  • [ ] Identify vitrectomy indications and timing
  • [ ] Manage laser/anti-VEGF complications
  • [ ] Coordinate combined modality treatment

2. Pan-Retinal Photocoagulation (PRP) — PDR Gold Standard

Indications (ETDRS)

  • High-Risk PDR (HRCs) — urgent
  • Early PDR (no HRC) — within 4–6 months
  • Severe NPDR (very severe, 4-2-1 rule) — consider if poor compliance/follow-up

Parameters (ETDRS Protocol)

ParameterValue
Burn size500 µm
Duration0.1 second
Power100–200 mW (barely visible grey-white burn)
Number of burns1200–1600 (2–3 sessions)
PatternMid-periphery to equator; spare macula (2 DD temporal), disc, major vessels
Spacing1 burn width apart

Efficacy (ETDRS)

  • 50% reduction in severe vision loss (<5/200) at 5 years
  • Regression of NVD/NVE in 60–80%
  • Recurrence if incomplete — repeat as needed

Complications

ComplicationIncidenceManagement
Peripheral visual field loss~20–30%May affect driving (DVLA standards)
Night vision lossCommonCounsel; avoid night driving if severe
DMO worsening15–30%Anti-VEGF ± focal laser; usually transient
Choroidal detachmentRare (<1%)Conservative; cycloplegia
Vitreous haemorrhageRareObserve; vitrectomy if non-clearing
PainDuring procedureTopical anaesthetic; sub-Tenon's if extensive

3. Focal/Grid Laser — Macular (Non-Centre DMO)

Indications

  • Non-centre-involving DMO (CSME outside 500µm from fovea)
  • Adjunct to anti-VEGF (Protocol I: modest injection reduction)

Parameters (ETDRS)

TypeParameters
Focal (microaneurysms)50–100 µm, 0.05–0.1s, 50–200mW; direct treatment of leaking MAs
Grid (diffuse leakage)50–100 µm, 0.05–0.1s, 50–200mW; 2 burn widths apart; avoid fovea (500µm)

Efficacy

  • 50% reduction in moderate visual loss (ETDRS)
  • Less used now for CI-DMO (anti-VEGF superior)
  • Still first-line for non-centre-involving DMO

4. Anti-VEGF Intravitreal Therapy

Agents & Dosing

AgentDoseVolumeRegimens
Aflibercept (Eylea)2mg0.05mLDMO: 5 monthly → 8w → 12w (T&E); PDR: monthly ×3 → PRN/T&E
Ranibizumab (Lucentis)0.5mg0.05mLDMO: 3 monthly → PRN/T&E; PDR: monthly ×3 → PRN
Bevacizumab (Avastin)1.25mg0.05mLOff-label; same regimens; cost-effective
Faricimab (Vabysmo)6mg0.05mLDual Ang-2/VEGF; 16-week interval potential

Indications by Disease

DiseaseAnti-VEGF Role
CI-DMOFirst-line (aflibercept preferred if VA ≤6/12)
Non-centre DMOAlternative if laser contraindicated
PDR (HRC)Alternative to PRP (Protocol S, CLARITY); less field loss
PDR (Early)Alternative; monitor for regression
Pre-proliferative + DMOTreat both simultaneously
Media opacity (cataract/VH)Only option (laser not possible)

DMO Protocols Comparison

ProtocolRegimenKey Feature
PRN (Pro Re Nata)Monthly ×3 → treat if CST >300µm or VA drop ≥5 lettersFewer injections; variable control
T&E (Treat & Extend)Monthly ×3 → extend 2–4w if stablePreferred — more consistent control; fewer visits
Fixed intervalMonthly / 8-weeklyHigh burden; guaranteed control

5. Dexamethasone Implant (Ozurdex)

FeatureDetail
Dose0.7mg biodegradable implant
Duration4–6 months sustained release
IndicationsAnti-VEGF inadequate; pseudophakic; vitrectomised; non-compliant
AdvantagesLong interval; no monthly visits; treats inflammation
ComplicationsCataract (phakic), IOP rise (30% >25mmHg), endophthalmitis

6. Vitrectomy — Indications & Timing

IndicationTimingProcedure
Non-clearing vitreous haemorrhage>3 months (or <3m if dense + no view for PRP)23/25G PPV; core vitrectomy ± PRP (endolaser) ± gas
Tractional RD (macula-on)Urgent (within days)Delamination ± endolaser ± gas/silicone oil
Tractional RD (macula-off)Within 1 weekAs above; lower visual prognosis
Combined TRD + rhegmatogenousUrgentBuckle + vitrectomy ± tamponade
Neovascular glaucoma (NVG)EmergencyPRP + IOP control → vitrectomy if angle closure
Ghost cell glaucomaUrgentVitrectomy + washout ± tamponade
EndophthalmitisEmergencyTap + inject ± vitrectomy

Tamponade Agents

AgentDurationUse
SF6 gas2–3 weeksStandard TRD
C3F8 gas6–8 weeksComplex/recurrent TRD
Silicone oilMonths (removal 3–6m)Complex TRD, proliferative vitreoretinopathy, patient non-compliant posturing

7. Combined / Sequential Therapy

ScenarioApproach
PDR + CI-DMOAnti-VEGF first (treats both); add PRP if NVD persistent
PDR + Non-centre DMOPRP + focal/grid laser
Post-vitrectomy PDREndolaser at surgery; anti-VEGF for residual DMO
Anti-VEGF tachyphylaxisSwitch agent (beva → aflibercept); add dexamethasone (pseudophakic); add laser

8. Complications Summary

TreatmentKey Complications
PRPField loss, night blindness, DMO worsening, choroidal detachment
Focal/GridParacentral scotoma (if too close to fovea), CNV (rare)
Anti-VEGFEndophthalmitis (0.05%), IOP spike, cataract, thromboembolic (theoretical), tachyphylaxis
DexamethasoneCataract, IOP rise, endophthalmitis
VitrectomyCataract progression (90% at 1y), retinal breaks, IOP issues, endophthalmitis, silicone oil emulsification

9. Exam Pearls (FCPS/MRCP)

TopicKey Point
PRP parameters500µm, 0.1s, 100–200mW, 1200–1600 burns
PRP efficacy50% ↓ severe vision loss (ETDRS)
PRP complicationDMO worsening (15–30%), peripheral field loss
Focal laserNon-centre DMO only; 50–100µm, 2 burn widths apart
Anti-VEGF DMOFirst-line CI-DMO; Aflibercept > Ranibizumab ≥ Bevacizumab
Anti-VEGF PDRAlternative to PRP; less field loss; needs frequent Rx; rebound risk
T&E regimenPreferred DMO regimen — extend interval if stable
Ozurdex0.7mg implant; 4–6 months; pseudophakic; IOP rise 30%
Vitrectomy: VHNon-clearing >3 months
Vitrectomy: TRDMacula-on urgent; macula-off within 1 week
NVGEmergency: PRP + IOP control + anti-VEGF

▸ 21.6.18 Diabetic Macular Oedema Dmo

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Differentiate centre-involving (CI-DMO) vs non-centre DMO
  • [ ] Apply OCT criteria for DMO diagnosis (central subfield thickness)
  • [ ] Select 1st line treatment: anti-VEGF for CI-DMO, laser for non-centre
  • [ ] Manage anti-VEGF treatment protocols (loading, treat-and-extend, PRN)
  • [ ] Recognise steroid implant (Ozurdex) as 2nd line

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
DefinitionRetinal thickening ≤1DD (disc diameter) from fovea due to VEGF-mediated vascular leakage
Prevalence~7% T1DM, ~5% T2DM; leading cause of vision loss in DR
Risk FactorsHbA1c, hypertension, dyslipidaemia, duration, pregnancy, renal disease
PathophysiologyHyperglycaemia → VEGF ↑ → vascular permeability → retinal oedema; BRB breakdown

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

PresentationFrequencyKey Features
Blurred visionMost commonCentral vision loss, distorted (metamorphopsia)
Reading difficultyCommonNeed brighter light, magnification
Colour desaturationLess commonWashed-out colours
AsymptomaticEarlyDetected on screening OCT

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

DMO Classification (ETDRS / OCT)

TypeDefinitionOCT CriteriaTreatment Priority
Centre-involving DMO (CI-DMO)Thickening ≤1DD from foveaCST >300–320µm (device-dependent)Anti-VEGF 1st line
Non-centre-involving DMOThickening >1DD from foveaCST normal; peripheral thickeningLaser if threatening centre

Severity (OCT-based)

SeverityCentral Subfield Thickness (CST)
Mild300–400µm
Moderate400–600µm
Severe>600µm

M-grading (Screening)

GradeDefinition
M0No DMO
M1DMO present (retinal thickening ≤1DD from fovea) → Urgent referral

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

InvestigationRoleKey Details
OCT (Optical Coherence Tomography)Gold standardCST measurement; cystoid spaces; subretinal fluid; monitor response
Digital photographyScreeningM1 if thickening ≤1DD from fovea
Fluorescein angiographyPre-laser / atypicalFocal leaks; capillary non-perfusion
HbA1c, BP, lipidsSystemic controlHbA1c <53; BP <130/80; fenofibrate

6. Differential Diagnosis

ConditionDistinguishing Features
Cystoid macular oedema (CMO)Post-surgery, uveitis, retinal vein occlusion; similar OCT
Age-related macular degeneration (AMD)Drusen, CNV, subretinal fluid; age >55; no DR
Central serous chorioretinopathy (CSCR)Young male, stress, serous detachment; no DR
Macular telangiectasiaBilateral, temporal to fovea, right-angle vessels

7. Management

Anti-VEGF Therapy (1st Line for CI-DMO)

AgentDoseLicensingRegimen
Ranibizumab0.5mg (0.05ml)Lucentis (licensed)Monthly ×3 → treat-and-extend / PRN
Aflibercept2mg (0.05ml)Eylea (licensed)Monthly ×3 → 8-weekly → treat-and-extend
Bevacizumab1.25mg (0.05ml)Avastin (off-label)Monthly ×3 → treat-and-extend / PRN
Loading phase: 3 monthly injections mandatory before extend/PRN
Treat-and-extend: Extend interval by 2–4 weeks if dry; shorten if fluid recurs
PRN (pro re nata): Re-treat if CST ↑>50µm or vision ↓>5 letters

Steroid Implant (2nd Line)

AgentDoseIndicationRisks
Dexamethasone implant (Ozurdex)700µgAnti-VEGF inadequate/refractoryIOP rise (30%), cataract progression

Laser (Non-centre DMO / Historical)

IndicationDetails
Non-centre DMOFocal/grid laser to leaking microaneurysms >1DD from fovea
Failed anti-VEGFConsider if persistent after 6+ injections

Systemic Control

TargetEvidence
HbA1c <53mmol/molDCCT/UKPDS: ↓DMO progression
BP <130/80UKPDS: tight BP ↓DR/DMO
FenofibrateFIELD/ACCORD-Eye: ↓DMO progression
flowchart TD
    A[DMO Detected] --> B{CI-DMO (CST >300µm, ≤1DD)?}
    B -->|Yes| C[Anti-VEGF 1st line]
    B -->|No| D[Laser if threatening centre]
    C --> E[Loading: 3 monthly]
    E --> F{Treat-and-extend or PRN?}
    F -->|T&E| G[Extend 2-4wk if dry]
    F -->|PRN| H[Re-treat if CST↑>50µm or vision↓>5 letters]
    G --> I[Review 4-8wk]
    H --> I
    I --> J{Inadequate response 6+ inj?}
    J -->|Yes| K[Dexamethasone implant (Ozurdex)]
    J -->|No| I

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
CI-DMO definitionRetinal thickening ≤1DD from fovea; CST >300–320µm
1st line treatmentAnti-VEGF (Ranibizumab 0.5mg, Aflibercept 2mg, Bevacizumab 1.25mg off-label)
Loading phase3 monthly injections before extend/PRN
Treat-and-extendExtend interval 2–4wk if dry; most common regimen
PRNRe-treat if CST ↑>50µm or vision ↓>5 letters
2nd lineDexamethasone implant (Ozurdex) — watch IOP rise
Non-centre DMOLaser (focal/grid)
SystemicHbA1c <53, BP <130/80, fenofibrate ↓progression

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
What is centre-involving DMO?Retinal thickening ≤1 disc diameter from fovea; CST >300–320µm on OCT
What is the 1st line treatment for CI-DMO?Anti-VEGF — Ranibizumab 0.5mg, Aflibercept 2mg, Bevacizumab 1.25mg (off-label); monthly ×3 loading then treat-and-extend/PRN
What is the difference between treat-and-extend and PRN?T&E: proactive extension by 2–4wk if dry; PRN: reactive re-treatment if CST↑>50µm or vision↓>5 letters
When do you use dexamethasone implant (Ozurdex)?Anti-VEGF inadequate after 6+ injections; monitor IOP (30% rise)
How does fenofibrate affect DMO?FIELD/ACCORD-Eye: fenofibrate ↓DMO progression; consider if dyslipidaemia
What is the ETDRS M1 grade?DMO on photography (thickening ≤1DD from fovea) → urgent referral

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
Bevacizumab unlicensed for DMO?YES — off-label; cost-effective; NICE allows if ranibizumab/aflibercept not suitable; same efficacy in trials
Laser for CI-DMO?NO — Anti-VEGF 1st line; laser for non-centre DMO only
OCT vs photography for DMO?OCT = gold standard (quantitative CST); photography = screening (M1 = refer)

Mnemonic: DMO-CI-AVE

  • DMO: thickening ≤1DD from fovea
  • M1 grade = DMO on photo → refer
  • OCT gold standard: CST >300-320µm
  • CI-DMO = centre-involving ≤1DD → Anti-VEGF 1st line
  • Intravitreal: Ranibizumab 0.5mg, Aflibercept 2mg, Bevacizumab 1.25mg
  • Loading: 3 monthly MUST
  • Anti-VEGF → treat-and-extend OR PRN
  • Treat-and-extend: extend 2-4wk if dry
  • PRN: retreat if CST↑>50 or vision↓>5
  • Extend: most common regimen
  • Ozurdex (dexamethasone implant) 2nd line: watch IOP
  • Non-centre DMO: laser
  • Systemic: HbA1c<53, BP<130/80, fenofibrate

▼  Show infographic image click to expand
▸ 21.6.19 Diabetic Retinopathy
[!info]
Diabetic retinopathy: leading cause of blindness in working-age adults — microvascular complication; ETDRS grading drives screening/referral; NPDR → PDR → DMO; Anti-VEGF 1st line for DMO; PRP for PDR; Annual screening (2-yearly if no DR).

1. Learning Objectives

  • [ ] Apply ETDRS grading for screening and referral decisions
  • [ ] Differentiate NPDR, PDR, and DMO features
  • [ ] State screening intervals by DR severity
  • [ ] Select treatment: anti-VEGF for DMO, PRP for PDR, vitrectomy for VH/TRD
  • [ ] Counsel on glycaemic/BP/lipid control for progression prevention

2. Definition & Epidemiology

FeatureDetail
DefinitionMicrovascular retinal damage from chronic hyperglycaemia → capillary occlusion, leakage, neovascularisation
PrevalenceAny DR: ~35% T1DM, ~25% T2DM; Vision-threatening DR: ~10% T1DM, ~5% T2DM
IncidenceT1DM: ~90% at 20y; T2DM: ~60% at 20y
Risk FactorsDuration of diabetes, HbA1c, hypertension, dyslipidaemia, pregnancy, nephropathy, anaemia
PathophysiologyPericyte loss → capillary basement membrane thickening → occlusion → ischaemia → VEGF → leakage + neovascularisation

3. Clinical Features / Presentation

PresentationFrequencyKey Features
AsymptomaticEarly NPDRNo visual symptoms; detected on screening
Blurred visionDMOCentral vision loss, distortion, reading difficulty
Floaters / sudden vision lossVitreous haemorrhage (PDR)Painless, "cobwebs", "curtain"
Visual field defectsTractional retinal detachmentPeripheral then central loss
Red Flags: Sudden vision loss (vitreous haemorrhage, TRD); rapid progression in pregnancy; new vessels on disc (NVD) or elsewhere (NVE).

4. Classification / Staging / Grading

ETDRS Grading Scale (Simplified)

ETDRS LevelRetinopathy StageKey FeaturesReferral Timing
10–12No DRNo abnormalitiesAnnual
20–35Mild–Moderate NPDRMicroaneurysms only (20); Haemorrhages ± exudates ± cotton wool (35)Annual / 6–12 months
43–53Severe NPDR4:2:1 rule: 4 quadrants haem, 2 venous beading, 1 IRMA3–6 months
61–65Early PDRNVD <1/3 disc area, NVE <1/2 disc area, no VHUrgent (2–4 weeks)
71–75High-risk PDRNVD ≥1/3 disc, NVE ≥1/2 disc, VH, pre-retinal haemUrgent (1–2 weeks)
M1Diabetic Macular Oedema (DMO)Retinal thickening ≤1DD from fovea (centre-involving)Urgent (2–4 weeks)
4:2:1 Rule (Severe NPDR): Haemorrhages/microaneurysms in 4 quadrants OR Venous beading in 2 quadrants OR Intraretinal microvascular abnormalities (IRMA) in 1 quadrant

DMO Classification

TypeDefinitionTreatment Priority
Centre-involving DMO (CI-DMO)Thickening ≤1DD from foveaAnti-VEGF 1st line
Non-centre-involving DMOThickening >1DD from foveaLaser (if threatening centre)

5. Diagnosis & Investigations

InvestigationRoleKey Details
Digital retinal photographyScreening (mydriatic 45°/2-field)Annual (2-yearly if no DR); quality assured
OCT (Optical Coherence Tomography)DMO diagnosis/monitoringCentral subfield thickness (CST); CI-DMO if CST >300–320µm (device-dependent)
Fluorescein angiographyPRP planning, NVE/NVD confirmationIf media opacity; OCT-A emerging alternative
HbA1c, BP, lipidsSystemic risk factor controlHbA1c <53mmol/mol; BP <130/80; LDL <1.8mmol/L

6. Differential Diagnosis

ConditionDistinguishing Features
Hypertensive retinopathyAV nicking, copper/silver wiring, flame haemorrhages; no microaneurysms, no neovascularisation
Retinal vein occlusionSectoral haemorrhages, disc oedema, venous dilatation; unilateral; no microaneurysms elsewhere
Radiation retinopathyDelayed (months-years post-RT); microaneurysms, capillary non-perfusion; history RT
Ocular ischaemic syndromeCarotid stenosis; mid-peripheral haemorrhages, neovascularisation of iris/angle; low IOP
Age-related macular degenerationDrusen, geographic atrophy, CNV; age >55; spares periphery

7. Management

Screening & Referral

DR StageScreening IntervalReferral
No DR (R0)2 years (UK NSC)None
Mild NPDR (R1)AnnualRoutine ophthalmology
Moderate NPDR (R2)6–12 monthsRoutine ophthalmology
Severe NPDR (R3)3–6 monthsUrgent (2–4 weeks)
PDR (R4)—Urgent (1–2 weeks)
DMO (M1)—Urgent (2–4 weeks)

Treatment

Indication1st Line2nd Line / AdjunctDetails
Centre-involving DMOAnti-VEGF (Ranibizumab 0.5mg, Aflibercept 2mg, Bevacizumab 1.25mg off-label)Dexamethasone implant (Ozurdex) if anti-VEGF inadequateMonthly loading ×3, then treat-and-extend / PRN
Non-centre DMOLaser (ETDRS grid)Anti-VEGF if threatening centreFocal/grid laser to leaking microaneurysms
PDR (Early/High-risk)PRP (Panretinal Photocoagulation)Anti-VEGF (adjunct for VH/NV regression)1200–1600 burns over 2–3 sessions; avoids macula
Vitreous haemorrhageObserve 3 months (if no PDR tx needed) → Vitrectomy if non-clearingAnti-VEGF pre-op to regress NVEarly vitrectomy if PDR + VH (DRVS)
Tractional RDVitrectomy—Urgent if macula threatened
Anti-VEGF agents: Ranibizumab (Lucentis, licensed), Aflibercept (Eylea, licensed), Bevacizumab (Avastin, off-label, cost-effective)
flowchart TD
    A[Screening Photo] --> B{ETDRS Grade}
    B -->|No DR (10-12)| C[2-yearly screen]
    B -->|Mild NPDR (20-35)| D[Annual screen]
    B -->|Mod NPDR (35-43)| E[6-12 month screen]
    B -->|Severe NPDR (43-53)| F[Urgent referral 2-4 weeks]
    B -->|PDR (61+)| G[Urgent referral 1-2 weeks]
    B -->|DMO (M1)| H[Urgent referral 2-4 weeks]
    F --> I[Ophthalmology: consider PRP if high-risk]
    G --> J[PRP + Anti-VEGF for NV]
    H --> K[Anti-VEGF 1st line (CI-DMO)]
    J --> K

Systemic Risk Factor Control

TargetEvidence
HbA1c <53mmol/mol (7.0%)DCCT/UKPDS: ↓DR onset/progression 30–40% per 1% HbA1c ↓
BP <130/80UKPDS: tight BP ↓DR progression 34%
Lipids: FenofibrateFIELD/ACCORD-Eye: fenofibrate ↓DR progression (esp NPDR); consider if dyslipidaemia
AspirinNo effect on DR; safe for CVD

8. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Points
ScreeningDigital photography; Annual (2-yearly if no DR); ETDRS grading
ETDRS referral thresholdsSevere NPDR (43–53): 2–4 weeks; PDR (61+): 1–2 weeks; DMO (M1): 2–4 weeks
4:2:1 RuleSevere NPDR: 4 quadrant haem OR 2 quadrant venous beading OR 1 quadrant IRMA
DMO treatmentCentre-involving: Anti-VEGF 1st line (Ranibizumab/Aflibercept/Bevacizumab); Non-centre: Laser
PDR treatmentPRP (1200–1600 burns); Anti-VEGF adjunct for NV/VH
Vitreous haemorrhageObserve 3mo → Vitrectomy if non-clearing; Early if PDR (DRVS)
Tractional RDVitrectomy urgent if macula threatened
Systemic controlHbA1c <53, BP <130/80, Fenofibrate for DR progression
PregnancyScreen each trimester; rapid progression risk

9. Viva Questions

QuestionExpected Answer
What is the ETDRS grading for severe NPDR?4:2:1 Rule: Haemorrhages/microaneurysms in 4 quadrants OR Venous beading in 2 quadrants OR IRMA in 1 quadrant (ETDRS levels 43–53)
Screening intervals by DR stage?No DR: 2-yearly; Mild NPDR: annual; Moderate NPDR: 6–12 months; Severe NPDR: 3–6 months; PDR/DMO: urgent referral
What is the 1st line treatment for centre-involving DMO?Anti-VEGF (Ranibizumab 0.5mg, Aflibercept 2mg, Bevacizumab 1.25mg off-label); monthly ×3 then treat-and-extend/PRN
What is the treatment for PDR?PRP (Panretinal Photocoagulation) — 1200–1600 burns over 2–3 sessions, sparing macula; Anti-VEGF adjunct for NV/VH
When do you refer severe NPDR urgently?ETDRS 43–53 (4:2:1 rule) → Urgent referralwithin 2–4 weeks
How does fenofibrate affect DR?FIELD/ACCORD-Eye: fenofibrate ↓DR progression (30–40%); consider in NPDR with dyslipidaemia
What is the difference between CI-DMO and non-CI DMO?CI-DMO: thickening ≤1DD from fovea → Anti-VEGF 1st line; Non-CI DMO: >1DD → Laser (if threatening centre)
Screening in pregnancy?Pre-conception, then each trimester; rapid progression risk; postpartum 6 months

10. Confusions & Mnemonics

ConfusionClarification
PRP for DMO?NO — PRP for PDR; Focal/grid laser for non-centre DMO; Anti-VEGF for centre-involving DMO
Anti-VEGF for PDR?Adjunct only (NV regression pre-PRP, VH); PRP is definitive
Bevacizumab unlicensed?Yes — off-label; cost-effective; NICE allows if ranibizumab/aflibercept not suitable

Mnemonic: DR-ETDRS-421

  • DR: leading cause blindness working age
  • Retinopathy: microaneurysms → haemorrhages → cotton wool → venous beading → IRMA → NVE/NVD → VH/TRD
  • ETDRS grading: 10-12 none, 20-35 mild/mod, 43-53 severe, 61+ PDR, M1 DMO
  • Two-yearly screen if no DR; Annual if mild; 6-12mo if mod; 3-6mo if severe
  • DMO: CI-DMO → Anti-VEGF 1st; Non-CI → Laser
  • Referral: Severe NPDR 2-4wk; PDR 1-2wk; DMO 2-4wk
  • Systemic: HbA1c<53, BP<130/80, Fenofibrate ↓progression
  • 4: 4 quadrant haemorrhages
  • 2: 2 quadrant venous beading
  • 1: 1 quadrant IRMA
  • = Severe NPDR (43-53) → Urgent referral

12. One-Page Revision Card

DomainKey Points
DefinitionMicrovascular retinal damage → leading cause blindness in working age
Key TestDigital retinal photography (annual); OCT for DMO; Fluorescein for PRP planning
ClassificationETDRS: 10-12 none; 20-35 mild/mod; 43-53 severe (4:2:1); 61+ PDR; M1 DMO
Acute MgmtVH: observe 3mo → vitrectomy; TRD: urgent vitrectomy
Chronic MgmtCI-DMO: Anti-VEGF; PDR: PRP; Systemic: HbA1c<53, BP<130/80, Fenofibrate
Key ScoreETDRS referral: Severe NPDR 2-4wk; PDR 1-2wk; DMO 2-4wk
ComplicationsVitreous haemorrhage, tractional RD, blindness
PrognosisGood with timely treatment; DMO responsive to anti-VEGF; PRP preserves central vision
